Title: Automated Reasoning with Nested Datatypes

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.30888v2

Published Time: Thu, 02 Jul 2026 00:31:53 GMT

Number of Pages: 63

Markdown Content:
# Automated Reasoning with Nested Datatypes 

Tomer Hakak 1, Yoni Zohar 1, Andrew Reynolds 2, Clark Barrett 3 and Cesare Tinelli 2  

> 1Bar-Ilan University, Ramat Gan, Israel 2The University of Iowa, Iowa City, USA 3Stanford University, Stanford, USA

Abstract 

> We introduce a theory of nested datatypes. The theory is obtained by restricting the naive combination of datatypes and arrays, so as to prevent non-standard models from emerging. A decision procedure for the theory is given and proven correct. Finally, we describe an implementation of the procedure, as well as an evaluation over both real-world and crafted benchmarks.

# 1. Introduction 

The SMT-LIB theory of datatypes allows for a direct and convenient encoding of common data structures, such as lists, trees, and more, which makes it useful for software verification. And indeed, the 2025 release of the SMT-LIB benchmark library includes over 50K benchmarks that involve datatypes [1]. The expressivity of datatypes is, however, limited. For example, there are no operators to update an existing datatype value, and there are no random-access operators. Such limitations call for the combination of datatypes with theories for other data structures, like arrays. A naive combination of datatypes and arrays introduces a problem: arrays bypass the principle of acyclicity, according to which a datatype value cannot occur in itself. Indeed, a datatype value could now occur as an element of an array, where the array is a field of the same value. Such behavior is undesirable, as it does not match the intuitive combination of arrays and datatypes, which only intends to increase expressivity, not break acyclicity. We refer to such models that admit cycles as non-standard models . A more detailed description of this issue, both intuitively and formally, is given in Example 15 and Figure 2 below. In this paper we define a theory of nested datatypes , obtained by restricting the aforementioned naive combination to exclude non-standard models. We introduce a decision procedure for the new theory, based on a reduction to the (naive) combination of datatypes, arrays and uninterpreted functions. We have implemented a prototype of our reduction-based algorithm in the state-of-the-art SMT-solver cvc5 [ 2], and evaluated it on benchmarks arising from the Move prover verification tool [ 3], and on synthetic benchmarks that control the “hidden cycles” in datatypes and arrays. Our experimental evaluation shows promise. On the organic benchmarks from the Move Prover, it is comparable to and sometimes outperforms an existing solver for nested datatypes implemented in Z3 [4]. On the synthetic benchmarks, it outperforms Z3 .In summary, our contributions are: (𝑖 ) The introduction of a theory of nested datatypes; (𝑖𝑖 ) A reduction-based decision procedure for the new theory; (𝑖𝑖𝑖 ) An implementation in cvc5 and an evaluation using both real-world and crafted benchmarks. 

Informal overview: We intuitively explain the problem using the first row of Figure 2. This motivating example will be formally introduced and developed as a running example throughout the paper. Here 𝑥 is a datatype value, while 𝑎 is an array whose elements are datatype values. The first row visually describes two simple constraints: 𝑥 is read from array 𝑎 at index 𝑖 , 𝑎 is a field of 𝑥 . Each constraint is harmless by itself. Together, however, they describe a cycle: following the array field of 𝑥 leads to 𝑎 , and reading 𝑎 at index 𝑖 leads back to 𝑥 . The naive combination of the theories of datatypes and arrays misses this because the cycle is split between the theories. The theory of Nested datatypes that we propose here reject the combined constraint. 

> SMT 2026, 24th International Workshop on Satisfiability Modulo Theories
> © 2026 Copyright for this paper by its authors. Use permitted under Creative Commons License Attribution 4.0 International (CC BY 4.0).

1Related work: The introduced theory of nested datatypes generalizes the SMT-LIB theory of algebraic datatypes [ 5 ] (see [ 6 , 7 ] for prominent decision procedures and [ 8] for a more recent one). The SMT solver Z3 [ 4] supports nested datatypes. However, to the best of our knowledge, the literature lacks a precise definition of the supported theory and the implemented algorithm. 

Outline: Section 2 goes over the necessary notions from first-order logic and Satisfiability Modulo Theories. In Section 3 we introduce the theory of nested datatypes. In Section 4 we present a decision procedure for this theory, and then in Section 5 we describe an implementation and evaluation of it. The correctness proofs for the decision procedure are quite involved and long. They are included in the appendix. 

# 2. Preliminaries 

For natural numbers 0 ≤ 𝑘 ≤ 𝑛 , we write [𝑘, 𝑛 ] to denote {𝑘, 𝑘 +1 , . . . , 𝑛 }. For sets 𝑋 and 𝑌 , AC (𝑋, 𝑌 )

is the set of almost constant functions from 𝑋 to 𝑌 , i.e., {𝑓 : 𝑋 → 𝑌 | ∃ 𝑐 ∈ 𝑌. ∃𝑆 ⊆ 𝑋. |𝑆 | <

∞ ∧ ∀ 𝑥 / ∈ 𝑆, 𝑓 (𝑥 ) = 𝑐 }. Given set 𝑋 and element 𝑒 , const 𝑋 (𝑒 ) is the constant function whose domain is 𝑋 and always returns 𝑒 . When 𝑋 is clear from the context, we omit it, writing const (𝑒 ).

Definition 1 (Well-founded relation). A binary relation 𝑅 on a set 𝑋 is well-founded if there is no infinite sequence 𝑥 0, 𝑥 1, 𝑥 2, . . . of elements of 𝑋 such that 𝑥 𝑛 +1 𝑅 𝑥 𝑛 for every 𝑛 .

2.1. Signatures and Structures 

We briefly review the usual definitions of many-sorted first-order logic with equality (see [ 9 , 10 ]). For any set 𝑆 , an 𝑆 -sorted set 𝐴 is a function from 𝑆 to 𝒫 (𝑋 ) ∖ {∅} for some set 𝑋 such that 𝐴 (𝑠 ) ∩ 𝐴 (𝑠 ′) = ∅

whenever 𝑠 ̸ = 𝑠 ′. We use 𝐴 𝑠 to denote 𝐴 (𝑠 ).A many-sorted signature Σ consists of a set 𝒮 Σ of sorts , a set ℱΣ of function symbols , and a set 𝒫 Σ of 

predicate symbols . Function symbols have arities 𝜎 1 × . . . × 𝜎 𝑛 → 𝜎 , and predicate symbols have arities 

𝜎 1 × . . . × 𝜎 𝑛 , with 𝜎 1, . . . , 𝜎 𝑛 , 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ. For each sort 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ, 𝒫 Σ includes an equality symbol =𝜎 of arity 𝜎 × 𝜎 , denoted = when 𝜎 is clear. Σ is called finite if 𝒮 Σ, ℱΣ, and 𝒫 Σ are finite. We assume a 

𝒮 Σ-sorted set of variables . Well-sorted terms, formulas, and literals are defined as usual. For a Σ-formula 

𝜑 and a sort 𝜎 , we denote the set of free variables in 𝜑 of sort 𝜎 by vars 𝜎 (𝜑 ). This notation naturally extends to vars 𝑆 (𝜑 ) when 𝑆 is a set of sorts. When 𝑆 = 𝒮 Σ we may omit it. Let 𝐵 ⊆ ℱ Σ be a set of function symbols. We denote by Σ|𝐵 the signature with the same sorts as Σ, no predicate symbols (except 

=𝜎 for 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ), and whose set of function symbols is 𝐵 .A Σ-literal is called flat if it has one of the following forms: 𝑥 = 𝑓 (𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑛 ), 𝑃 (𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑛 ), or 

¬𝑃 (𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑛 ) for some variables 𝑥, 𝑦, 𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑛 and function and predicate symbols 𝑓 and 𝑃 from 

Σ. A (flat) cube is a conjunction of (flat) literals. A Σ-structure consists of a 𝒮 Σ-sorted set 𝐴 , and interpretations to the function and predicate symbols of Σ. We further require that =𝜎 is interpreted as the identity relation over 𝐴 𝜎 for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ. A 

Σ-interpretation 𝒜 is an extension of a Σ-structure with interpretations to some set of variables. For any 

Σ-term 𝛼 , 𝛼 𝒜 denotes the interpretation of 𝛼 in 𝒜 . When 𝑋 is a set of Σ-terms, 𝑋 𝒜 ={︀ 𝑥 𝒜 | 𝑥 ∈ 𝑋 }︀ .Similarly, 𝑓 𝒜 and 𝑃 𝒜 denote the interpretation of 𝑓 and 𝑃 in 𝒜 , and we denote 𝐴 𝜎 by 𝜎 𝒜 for every 

𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ. Given a signature Σ′ which is contained in Σ, we denote by 𝒜 Σ′

the restriction of 𝒜 to Σ′.Satisfaction is denoted by |=. A Σ-theory 𝑇 is a class of Σ-structures. A Σ-interpretation whose variable-free part is in 𝑇 is a 𝑇 -interpretation . 𝜑 is 𝑇 -satisfiable (denoted |=𝑇 𝜑 ) if 𝒜 | = 𝜑 for some 

𝑇 -interpretation 𝒜 . 𝜑 and 𝜙 are 𝑇 -equivalent if they are satisfied by the same class of 𝑇 -interpretations. The Σ-theory of equality and uninterpreted functions (EUF) is the class of all Σ-structures. A signature 

Σ is called an arrays signature if 𝒮 Σ = {𝐴, 𝐼, 𝐸 } for distinct sorts 𝐴 , 𝐼 , and 𝐸 , ℱΣ = {select 𝐴 , store 𝐴 },and 𝒫 Σ is empty. The arity of select 𝐴 is 𝐴 → 𝐸 , and the arity of store 𝐴 is (𝐴 × 𝐼 × 𝐸 ) → 𝐴 . When 𝐴 

is clear from context, we may omit it, writing select and store . For an array signature Σ, we say that a 

> 2

Σ-structure 𝒜 is an arrays Σ-structure if it satisfies the axioms for arrays as specified in SMT-LIB. The 

Σ-theory of arrays is the class of all arrays Σ-structures (see, e.g. [11]). 

2.2. The SMT-LIB 2 Theory of Datatypes 

We adapt the definition from [12] of the SMT-LIB theory of datatypes [5].

Selectors are often renamed, e.g., linked lists are often modeled using constructors nil and cons ,where the two selectors for cons are named hd and tl , rather than 𝑠 (cons ,0) and 𝑠 (cons ,1) .We will use the signatures in Table 1 as running examples. The signature Σ0 is a pure datatypes signature with element sort 𝐼 and two struct sorts, 𝐴 and 𝐸 . In Σ0, the sort 𝐴 is a list sort for 𝐸 -elements, built using nil and cons . The sort 𝐸 is built using the constructor c, whose selectors are id and chld ;intuitively, id stores an index of sort 𝐼 , and chld stores the list of children of sort 𝐴 . The signature Σ1

uses the same sort names for arrays from 𝐼 to 𝐸 , with array sort 𝐴 . Thus, 𝐴 is not meant to be both a datatype list sort and an array sort inside one component theory: it is a list sort in Σ0 and an array sort in Σ1. The signature Σ− 

> 2

keeps only the datatype constructor c, and treats sort 𝐴 as an element sort for datatypes. Σ2 extends it with the constant constructor emp : 𝐸 , used as an empty 𝐸 -value. Finally, Σ−

𝑇 𝜎 (Σ , 𝐴 ) contains the ground 𝜎 -sorted terms over Σ with 𝐴 as constants.

2.3. Decidability and Combination 

A Σ-theory is decidable if the set of 𝒯 -satisfiable flat Σ-cubes is decidable; arrays, EUF, and datatypes are decidable (see, e.g., [ 11 ]). Given signatures Σ1, Σ2 and theories 𝑇 1, 𝑇 2 over them, Σ1 ∪ Σ2 is the union of

EUF and arrays are stably infinite w.r.t. all their sorts (see, e.g., [ 11 ]); for a datatypes signature Σ, 𝒯 D𝑇  

> Σ

is stably infinite w.r.t. Elem Σ (see, e.g., [ 6]). The Nelson-Oppen (NO) method [ 14 , 10 ] decides satisfiability in the combination of stably infi-nite theories, by reasoning about arrangements (conjunctions of (dis)equalities) between shared variables. 

# 3. The Theory of Nested datatypes 

In this section we define the theory of nested datatypes. There are two challenges in doing so, one syntactic, and the other semantic. The first invites a new definition for a well-foundedness of a sort, that must take into account the existence of arrays. The second requires to exclude unwanted structures from the defined theory. 

3.1. First challenge: well-foundedness 

Going back to Table 1, notice that 𝜙 0 uses the theory 𝒯 D𝑇  

> Σ0

of datatypes to represent a nested structure. This is natural, but sometimes not expressive enough. For example, 𝒯 D𝑇  

> Σ0

does not have an operator to update such lists, or a random-access reading operator. Thus, it might be desirable to have the second field of datatype 𝐸 be an array of datatypes, rather than a list, as the next example does. 

Example 9. Consider signature Σ−

> ndt

Noticeably, Σ− 

> ndt

no longer has the constructor nil , that was available in Σ0. There, it was used as an empty list constructor. However, we have now replaced lists with arrays, and there are no empty arrays. But now we have a problem. The well-foundedness condition from Definition 2 that was ensured by 

nil in Example 4 is now lacking: in terms of Definition 2, 𝐺 1 will no longer include the sort 𝐴 , and so 𝐺 2 will not include the sort 𝐸 . Thus, replacing lists by arrays requires an appropriate definition of well-foundedness, that explicitly incorporates arrays sorts. 

Definition 10 (Well-founded sort (NDT)). Let Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 be arrays signatures, each with 𝒮 Σ𝑘 =

{𝐴 𝑘 , 𝐼 𝑘 , 𝐸 𝑘 } for each 1 ≤ 𝑘 ≤ 𝑛 , and let Σ𝑛 +1 be a datatypes signature. Let Σ =⋃︀ 𝑛 +1  

> 𝑘 =1

𝜎, 𝜎 1, . . . , 𝜎 𝑛 ∈ 𝐹 𝑖 (Σ , 𝑆 )} ∪ { 𝐴 𝑘 | 𝐸 𝑘 , 𝐼 𝑘 ∈ 𝐹 𝑖 (Σ , 𝑆 )}. A sort 𝜎 is well-founded in Σ w.r.t. 𝑆 if

Notice that while in Definition 2 well-foundedness is defined for datatypes signatures, in Definition 10 it is defined for signatures obtained by a union of a datatypes signature with a number of array signatures.

We shall also require all array sorts to be distinct from one another and from the datatype sorts. We further assume that the nesting of arrays and datatypes only involves the element sort of arrays, not the index sort; and that there is no nesting between arrays. This is formalized as follows: 

Notation 1. Let Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 be array signatures, s.t. 𝒮 Σ𝑗 = {𝐴 𝑗 , 𝐼 𝑗 , 𝐸 𝑗 } for all 1 ≤ 𝑗 ≤ 𝑛 . Let Σ𝑛 +1 be a signature s.t. Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 +1 are pairwise disjoint. For Σ := ⋃︀ 𝑛 +1

Definition 12 (NDT signature). Let Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 be arrays signatures, each with 𝒮 Σ𝑖 = { 𝐸 𝑖 , 𝐼 𝑖 , 𝐴 𝑖 },and let Σ𝑛 +1 be a datatypes signature and denote 𝒮 Σ𝑛 +1 = Elem Σ𝑛 +1 ⊎Struct Σ𝑛 +1 . Let Σ = ⋃︀ 𝑛 +1  

> 𝑘 =1

|Array Σ| = 𝑛 , {𝐼 1, . . . , 𝐼 𝑛 } ∩ (Struct Σ𝑛 +1 ∪ Array Σ) = ∅, and Struct Σ𝑛 +1 ∩ Array Σ = ∅.Then Σ is called the NDT signature of Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 +1 .

3.2. Second challenge: non-standard interpretations 

Let us start with the most natural candidate as semantics for nested datatypes, obtained by simply taking the theory combination of datatypes and arrays.

-structures, and require its well-foundedness. The relation connects input and output sorts of both arrays and datatypes. Its formal definition is quite involved, but intuitively it simply relates terms to their sub-terms and arrays to their elements. With this relation, we can finally define the theory of nested datatypes that we will adopt.

# 4. Decision Procedure for Nested Datatypes 

In this section we introduce a decision procedure for the theory of nested datatypes. In what follows, let Σ

be the NDT signature of Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 +1 , where Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 are arrays signatures and Σ𝑛 +1 is a datatypes signature. Also, let 𝜑 be a Σ-formula. Our procedure will determine the 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-satisfiability of 𝜑 .The procedure works as follows: we define a new signature Σ𝜑 and a Σ𝜑 -formula trFormula (𝜑 )

such that 𝜑 is 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-satisfiable if and only if trFormula (𝜑 ) is satisfiable in the Σ𝜑 -theory obtained by combining the theories of datatypes, arrays, and uninterpreted functions (but without the restriction to NDT structures). The latter can be decided using existing SMT-solvers for the resulting combination, in a Nelson-Oppen fashion. 

4.1. Translation of Signatures and Theories 

We begin by defining new sorts. The idea is to have the theories of arrays and datatypes separated, so that datatypes will not have array sorts as element sorts. The connection between the datatypes and the arrays from the original signature will be maintained by uninterpreted functions. For every sort 𝜎 in 𝒮 Σ we define a new sort 𝜎 *. For each array sort 𝐴 in Array Σ, we introduce two additional sorts: 𝐴 ′ and 𝐴 ∘. In total, each sort 𝐴 in Array Σ gives rise to three new sorts: 𝐴 *, 𝐴 ′ and 𝐴 ∘;and each other sort 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ ∖ Array Σ gives rise to a single new sort 𝜎 *. The new sorts are summarized in Table 3. Intuitively, for each non-array sort 𝜎 , we create a copy of it named 𝜎 *. But if 𝜎 is an array sort, 

𝜎 * is no longer a copy of it. Instead, it is a datatypes sort that is meant to represent the original array sort. Additionally, 𝜎 ′ is an array sort, and its element sort may represent a datatype, but not a nested one. Thus, every array sort 𝜎 is represented by two sorts: 𝜎 * and 𝜎 ′. The sort 𝜎 * will be used only for checking cycles, whereas 𝜎 ′ is used for the regular array reasoning. Finally, 𝜎 ∘ is an additional sort that serves as a technical separator between datatypes with sort 𝜎 *. Then, the new signature Σ𝜑 is defined as follows.

is given in Table 4,

Remark 25. It might be more natural to represent each array variable 𝑎 with its own constructor 𝑐 𝑎 ,thus omitting the first argument (and the sort 𝐴 ∘ altogether). However, we want to make sure that if our input formula entails an equality 𝑎 = 𝑏 between two array variables 𝑎 and 𝑏 , then the datatype terms that represent them will also be equal. But if these datatypes are constructed using distinct constructors, they will surely be distinct. In contrast, having an identifier as an argument to the constructor, allows us to keep the resulting datatypes equal.

4.3. Lemmas 

To fix the issue raised by Example 27, we augment tr with lemmas, that are given in Table 10. Recall that each constructor 𝑐 𝑗 has an arity based on the set I(𝐴 𝑗 ,𝜑 ). In order to properly apply the constructors 𝑐 𝑗 s, we fix an ordering for the set I(𝐴 𝑗 ,𝜑 ) by fixing a bijection: 

Definition 28. Given 1 ≤ 𝑗 ≤ 𝑛 and 𝜑 , 𝑜 (𝐴 𝑗 ,𝜑 ) denotes a fixed bijection from I(𝐴 𝑗 ,𝜑 ) to {1, . . . , 𝑛 (𝐴 𝑗 ,𝜑 )}.When 𝐴 𝑗 and 𝜑 are clear from the context, we omit them and simply write 𝑜 .

L𝜑 

0(𝑎, 𝑖 ) ensures that the value obtained by a select on the array variable 𝑎 ′ of sort 𝐴 ′

𝑗 at index 𝑖 * agrees with the corresponding field of 𝑎 *. It utilizes variables 𝑢 (𝑎, 1) , . . . , 𝑢 (𝑎,𝑛 (𝐴 𝑖 ,𝜑 )) to keep the translation 

10 flat. L1(𝜑 ) applies L0 to every select literal in 𝜑 , while L2(𝜑 ) applies it for all indices occurring in the formula and every array variable that appears under a store. The motivation for L3(𝜑 ) is to ensure that for every array sort 𝐴 , there is a one to one mapping between the interpretations of free variables of sort 𝐴 ′ and those of sort 𝐴 *. 𝑓 𝐴 and 𝑔 𝐴 are asserted to be inverses of one another when restricted to the relevant domain, thus ensuring the 1-1 correspondence. We now combine these sets of literals to obtain the full set of lemmas associated with the formula 𝜑 .This allows us to define the final translation function trFormula .

Definition 29. Let 𝜑 be a flat cube in an NDT signature Σ. The set of lemmas associated with 𝜑 is L(𝜑 ) = 

L1(𝜑 ) ∪ L2(𝜑 ) ∪ L3(𝜑 ). The translation trFormula is defined by: trFormula (𝜑 ) = tr (𝜑 ) ∧⋀︀ L(𝜑 ).

4.4. Correctness 

The next theorem shows that our translation is correct, in the sense that it preserves (un)satisfiability, assuming a stable infiniteness condition. 

Theorem 31. Suppose 𝑇 𝑛 +1 is stably infinite w.r.t. (︀⋃︀ 𝑛 𝑖 =1 𝒮 Σ𝑖 )︀ ∩ 𝒮 Σ𝑛 +1 . Then 𝜑 is 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-satisfiable iff 

trFormula (𝜑 ) is 𝑇 𝜑 

> new

-satisfiable. The left-to-right direction holds without assuming stable infiniteness. 

Given that the translation is correct and is easily computable, it is only left to show that the target theory is decidable, which holds under the same stable infiniteness assumptions. 

Theorem 32. Suppose 𝑇 𝑛 +1 is stably infinite w.r.t. (︀⋃︀ 𝑛 𝑖 =1 𝒮 Σ𝑖 )︀ ∩ 𝒮 Σ𝑛 +1 Then 𝑇 𝜑  

> new

is decidable.

and Σ2. Let 𝑇 1, 𝑇 2 be the array theory over Σ1 and the Σ2-theory of datatypes; 𝑇 2 is stably infinite w.r.t. 

{𝐼, 𝐴 } (see Section 2.3). By Theorems 31 and 32, 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σndt

is decidable. 

# 5. Implementation and Evaluation 

A prototype implementation of the translation is implemented as a preprocessing pass in the cvc5 SMT solver [ 2 ]. Although the reduction is defined over flat cubes, the implementation handles arbitrary input formulas by applying the translation function trFormula recursively to all subterms. 

5.1. Setup 

All experiments were conducted on a machine running Ubuntu 22.04 via WSL2 (Windows Subsystem for Linux 2, kernel 6.6.87.2-microsoft-standard-WSL2 ), equipped with a 13th Gen Intel Core i7-1355U processor (4 cores, 8 logical CPUs) and 11 GiB of RAM. A time limit of 10 seconds was imposed per benchmark. We used Z3 version 4.15.6 with default options, and our fork of cvc5 , invoked with the options to support our implementation. 2

5.2. Benchmarks 

We evaluated our reduction on two independent benchmark suites: 

Synthetic cycle benchmarks: formulas that systematically generalize 𝜙 from Table 1. For each 𝑘 ≥ 1,the formula 𝜓 (𝑘 ) is given in Figure 4. It encodes the existence of a cycle of size 2𝑘 , where half of the cycle goes through arrays and half through datatypes. Notice that 𝜓 (1) is the same as 𝜙 from Table 1. For every 𝑘 , 𝜓 (𝑘 ) is not satisfiable in the theory of nested datatypes. In order to test our implementation on satisfiable formulas, we generate satisfiable variants by removing a single constraint from 𝜓 (𝑘 ), which corresponds to breaking the cycle. These are formulas 𝜓 ′(𝑘 ) from Figure 4. We have generated 𝜓 (𝑘 ) for 1 ≤ 𝑘 ≤ 500 . The satisfiable instances were much more difficult for the solvers, and hence we generated 𝜓 ′(𝑘 ) for 1 ≤ 𝑘 ≤ 50 . This smaller bound is already beyond the largest 

𝑘 either solver can solve. 

Move Prover benchmarks: verification benchmarks that originate from the Move Prover [ 3]. The Move Prover [ 3 ] is a formal verification tool for smart contracts written in the Move language [ 15 ]. It works by encoding smart contracts, as well as verification conditions for them, in the Boogie intermediate verification language [ 16 ], which then generates SMT-LIB queries that are sent to SMT-solvers. 3 In order to correctly model persistent data structures, the encoding employs the combination of datatypes and arrays, where the intended meaning is that of nested datatypes. 4

We considered 15 tests from the Move Library. These tests use features that our translation does not support, like quantifier reasoning, incremental solving and constant arrays. Therefore, we performed the following steps using a script (available in the artifact): 1. Deleted top-level commands that use unsupported features, namely quantifiers and constant arrays. 2. Inlined all incremental check-sat 

calls by tracking the push and pop commands and generating SMT-LIB files that contain all relevant definitions and assertions in each context. 3. Removed options specific to Z3 and Boogie labels, which are automatically produced by Boogie. Concretely, across the 15 original benchmarks, the preprocessing considered 13,313 top-level SMT-LIB commands and removed 4029 of them: 2832 containing quantifiers, 236 containing constant arrays, 944 

Z3 options, and 17 Boogie label commands. The remaining 9284 commands were then split according to the incremental contexts. This way, we generated 76 non-incremental quantifier-free SMT-LIB files (51 satisfiable and 25 unsatisfiable), with an average of about 5 non-incremental benchmarks for each original incremental benchmark. In the Move prover, some nested datatypes include arrays whose index sort is a (nested) datatype, violating the assumption Struct Σ𝑛 +1 ∩ Array Σ = ∅ of Definition 12. This assumption was introduced to simplify the presentation, and our implementation supports such nested datatypes. The reason is that nested datatypes appearing in array indices are handled as a separate NDT signature from the one used for array elements. Thus, cycles through array elements are still detected by the reduction described in

this paper, while the nested structure of the index sort is solved by an independent NDT component. Sorts that cannot participate in such cycles, such as integers, may still be shared between these components; the remaining interaction is then handled by standard theory-combination techniques. While the Move prover benchmarks originate from a real-world application, they represent only a part of that application, as unsupported features are removed. Still, including them is relevant because the resulting benchmarks represent the basic quantifier-free non-incremental reasoning necessary (though not sufficient) for the original benchmarks. 

5.3. Results 

Table 11 summarizes the results, in terms of number of benchmarks that were solved within the time limit, and the total runtimes (in seconds) over the benchmarks that were commonly solved by both cvc5 

and Z3 . cvc5 is able to solve all benchmarks from the unsatisfiable synthetic set, and Z3 can solve most of them. On the commonly solved benchmarks, cvc5 is faster, and for most instances is able to solve the formula in under 2 seconds. The satisfiable synthetic benchmarks are harder for both solvers. cvc5 

solves half of them, while Z3 only solves one. Both solvers are able to solve all Move Prover benchmarks quickly, though cvc5 is slightly faster on the satisfiable ones. 

# 6. Conclusion 

We have introduced and studied a theory of nested datatypes, that avoids non-standard models. A decision procedure was presented, which is based on a translation to the standard combination between arrays and datatypes, augmented with uninterpreted functions. Our empirical evaluation supports the decision procedure and shows its effectiveness on both real-world and synthetic benchmarks. Next immediate steps is to expand the theoretical and practical support of our framework to incremental solving and quantifiers. More generally, he desired combination of arrays and datatypes is just a particular instance of a wider problem with naive theory combination. Indeed, similar undetected cycles can appear when replacing arrays with sequences, sets, and other data structures. A more general solution is therefore needed. We are hopeful that the particular case of arrays that we have solved here can serve as a basis for a more general solution.

# Appendix 

In this appendix we prove the main theoretical results of the paper, namely Theorem 31 (correctness) and Theorem 32 (decidability). For Theorem 31, we split the proof for soundness and completeness. Soundness is proven in Appendix A, and individually stated as Theorem 35. Completeness is proven in Appendix B, and individually stated as Theorem 53. The proof of Theorem 32 is presented in Appendix C. We shall make use of the following classical result: 

Theorem 34 (Nelson–Oppen [14, 10]). Let 𝑇 1 and 𝑇 2 be decidable Σ1- and Σ2-theories, respectively, such that: (i) ℱΣ1 ∩ ℱ Σ2 = ∅ and 𝒫 Σ1 ∩ 𝒫 Σ2 = ∅; and (ii) 𝑇 1 and 𝑇 2 are both stably infinite w.r.t. 

𝑆 0 = 𝒮 Σ1 ∩ 𝒮 Σ2 . Then 𝑇 1 ⊕ 𝑇 2 is decidable. 

# A. Soundness 

In what follows, let Σ be the NDT signature of Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 +1 , where Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 are arrays signatures and Σ𝑛 +1 is a datatypes signature. We prove the following soundness theorem: 

Theorem 35. For every flat Σ-cube 𝜑 , if 𝜑 is 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-satisfiable, then trFormula (𝜑 ) is 𝑇 𝜑 

> new

-satisfiable. 

As a motivating example, the formula 𝜙 from Table 1 is 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-unsatisfiable, and thus cannot be used for the current section. We therefore use the following 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-satisfiable example instead.

Lemma 41. The function h is a total function over ⋃︀ 𝜎 ∈𝒮 Σ 𝜎 𝒜 .Proof : Suppose, towards a contradiction, that there exists some 𝑡 ∈⋃︀ 𝜎 ∈𝒮 Σ 𝜎 𝒜 such that h(𝑡 ) is not defined. We know that 𝑡 / ∈ Struct Σ𝑛 +1 ∪ Array Σ, since otherwise h(𝑡 ) would be defined by the third case of Definition 39. Thus, there are 2 possibilities: 1. 𝑡 is of sort 𝜎 ∈ Struct Σ𝑛 +1 . Then, since 𝒜 Σ𝑛 +1 is a datatypes Σ𝑛 +1 -interpretation, 𝑡 must be of the form 𝑐 (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑚 ) for some constructor 𝑐 ∈ 𝒞𝒪 Σ𝑛 +1 and 𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑚 ∈ 𝜎 𝒜 . So there must be some 𝑡 𝑗 such that h(𝑡 𝑗 ) is not defined. Note that (𝑡 𝑗 , 𝑡 ) ∈ ℛ 𝒜 .2. 𝑡 is of sort 𝐴 𝑗 for some 𝑗 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ]. Then, there must be some 𝑖 ∈ I(𝐴 𝑗 ,𝜑 ) such that h(select 𝒜  

> 𝐴 𝑗

(𝑡, 𝑖 𝒜 )) 

is not defined. Note that (select 𝒜  

> 𝐴 𝑗

(𝑡, 𝑖 𝒜 ), 𝑡 ) ∈ ℛ 𝒜 .In either case, there exists some 𝑠 ∈⋃︀ 𝜎 ∈𝒮 Σ 𝜎 𝒜 such that (𝑠, 𝑡 ) ∈ ℛ 𝒜 and h(𝑠 ) is not defined. We can apply this argument again and get an infinitely descending sequence of elements in ℛ𝒜 , which contradicts the well-foundedness of ℛ𝒜 . □

Lemma 46. ΓDT is injective. Proof : We show first that if 𝑡 1 ∈ 𝜎 𝒜  

> 1

and 𝑡 2 ∈ 𝜎 𝒜  

> 2

with 𝜎 1̸ = 𝜎 2, then ΓDT (𝑡 1)̸ = Γ DT (𝑡 2). Indeed, by Lemma 43 ΓDT (𝑡 1) ∈ 𝜎 *  

> 1ℬ

and ΓDT (𝑡 2) ∈ 𝜎 * 

> 2ℬ

, and the domains for different new sorts are disjoint (see Table 14). It remains to prove that for each fixed sort 𝜎 , whenever 𝑡 1, 𝑡 2 ∈ 𝜎 𝒜 and 𝑡 1̸ = 𝑡 2, then ΓDT (𝑡 1)̸ =ΓDT (𝑡 2). We proceed by induction on 𝑁 := max {︀ ℎ(𝑡 1), ℎ (𝑡 2)}︀ ,

Inductive step. Assume the claim holds for all pairs whose maximum depth is < 𝑁 . Let 𝑡 1, 𝑡 2 ∈ 𝜎 𝒜 

with max {ℎ(𝑡 1), ℎ (𝑡 2)} = 𝑁 > 0. There are two cases to consider: 1. If 𝜎 ∈ Struct Σ𝑛 +1 , then there are two options: (a) If 𝑡 1 = 𝑐 (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑚 ) and 𝑡 2 = ^ 𝑐 (𝑟 1, . . . , 𝑟 𝑘 ) with 𝑐 ̸ = ^ 𝑐 , then according to Remark 44, we have 

ΓDT (𝑡 1) = 𝑐 *(. . . )̸ = ^ 𝑐 *(. . . ) = Γ DT (𝑡 2),

since distinct constructors yield disjoint images. (b) Otherwise both use the same constructor 𝑐 , say 𝑡 1 = 𝑐 (𝑠 1, . . . , 𝑠 𝑚 ) and 𝑡 2 = 𝑐 (𝑟 1, . . . , 𝑟 𝑚 ),but 𝑡 1̸ = 𝑡 2. Then for some 𝑗 , 𝑠 𝑗 ̸ = 𝑟 𝑗 and ℎ(𝑠 𝑗 ), ℎ (𝑟 𝑗 ) < 𝑁 . By the inductive hypoth-esis ΓDT (𝑠 𝑗 )̸ = Γ DT (𝑟 𝑗 ). Thus, due to Remark 44, ΓDT (𝑡 1) = 𝑐 *(. . . , ΓDT (𝑠 𝑗 ), . . . )̸ =

𝑐 *(. . . , ΓDT (𝑟 𝑗 ), . . . ) = Γ DT (𝑡 2).2. If 𝜎 = 𝐴 𝑗 ∈ Array Σ, then

Lemma 49. ΓArr is injective. Proof : Let 𝑡 1 ∈ 𝐴 𝒜  

> 𝑗

and 𝑡 2 ∈ 𝐴 𝒜  

> 𝑘

with 𝑗, 𝑘 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ], such that 𝑡 1̸ = 𝑡 2.

> 𝑗

and 𝑡 2 ∈ 𝐴 𝒜  

> 𝑘

> 𝑗

∧ ΓArr (𝑡 2) ∈ 𝐴 ′ℬ  

> 𝑘

. These are disjoint domains by construction, so ΓArr (𝑡 1)̸ = Γ Arr (𝑡 2).

Case 2: 𝑗 = 𝑘 , but 𝑡 1̸ = 𝑡 2. Then 𝑡 1, 𝑡 2 ∈ 𝐴 𝒜  

> 𝑗

. Since 𝒜 satisfies extensionality for arrays, there exists some index 𝑖 ∈ 𝐼 𝒜  

> 𝑗

= 𝐼 *  

> 𝑗 ℬ

such that 

select 𝒜  

> 𝐴 𝑗

(𝑡 1, 𝑖 )̸ = select 𝒜  

> 𝐴 𝑗

(𝑡 2, 𝑖 ).

> 𝐴 𝑗

> 𝐴 𝑗

Hence ΓArr (𝑡 1)̸ = Γ Arr (𝑡 2) in both cases. This proves that ΓArr is injective. □

# B. Completeness 

In what follows, Σ continues to be the NDT signature of Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 +1 , where Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 are arrays signatures and Σ𝑛 +1 is a datatypes signature. Now we can prove that the domains of the sorts in Σ are non-empty. 

Lemma 60. For every sort 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ, the domain 𝜎 𝒜 is nonempty. Proof : Let 𝑆 = 𝒮 Σ ∖(︀ Array Σ ∪ Struct Σ𝑛 +1 )︀ . Since all the sorts in 𝒮 Σ are well-founded with respect to 𝑆 , for each 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ there is some 𝑘 ∈ N with 𝜎 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 (Σ , 𝑆 ) (see Definition 10). We prove by induction on 𝑘 that ∀𝜎 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 (Σ , 𝑆 ). 𝜎 𝑘 ̸ = ∅.

Base case ( 𝑘 = 0 ). Then 𝜎 ∈ 𝐹 0(Σ , 𝑆 ) = 𝑆 , and by definition 𝜎 0 = 𝜎 *ℬ , which is nonempty, as ℬ is an interpretation. 

Inductive step. Assume the claim holds for 𝑘 . Take any 𝜎 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 +1 (Σ , 𝑆 ). By definition 

𝐹 𝑘 +1 = 𝐹 𝑘 ∪{︀ 𝜎 | ∃ 𝑐 : 𝜎 1 × · · · × 𝜎 𝑛 → 𝜎, 𝜎 𝑖 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 }︀ ∪ { 𝐴 𝑖 | 𝐼 𝑖 , 𝐸 𝑖 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 }.

There are three cases: 1. If 𝜎 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 , then 𝜎 𝑘 ̸ = ∅ by IH, so according to Lemma 59, 𝜎 𝑘 +1 ⊇ 𝜎 𝑘 ̸ = ∅.2. If there exists a constructor 𝑐 : 𝜎 1 × · · · × 𝜎 𝑛 → 𝜎 with each 𝜎 𝑖 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 , then IH gives 𝜎 𝑘 𝑖 ̸ = ∅.Hence {︀ 𝑐 (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑛 ) | 𝑡 𝑖 ∈ 𝜎 𝑘 𝑖 }︀ ⊆ 𝑇 𝜎 (Σ |𝒞𝒪 , 𝐵 𝑘 ) ⊆ 𝜎 𝑘 +1 , so 𝜎 𝑘 +1 ̸ = ∅.3. If 𝜎 = 𝐴 𝑖 is an array sort where 𝐸 𝑖 , 𝐼 𝑖 ∈ 𝐹 𝑘 , then IH gives 𝐼 𝑘 𝑖 ̸ = ∅ and 𝐸 𝑘 𝑖 ̸ = ∅. Therefore: 

𝐴 𝑘 +1  

> 𝑖

= AC (𝐼 𝑘 𝑖 , 𝐸 𝑘 𝑖 )̸ = ∅.Hence for each 𝜎 there is some 𝑘 with 𝜎 𝑘 ̸ = ∅, and so 𝜎 𝒜 =⋃︀ 𝑘 ∈N 𝜎 𝑘 is nonempty. □

B.4. Infiniteness of Each 𝐸 𝒜 

> 𝑖

In order to prove that each 𝐸 𝒜  

> 𝑖

is infinite, we first define the directed graph 𝐺 Σ = ( 𝑉, 𝐸 ) over the sorts of Σ, such that the edges go either from an output sort of a constructor into one of its input sorts, or from an array sort to its corresponding element sort. Formally: 

Definition 63. Let Σ be an NDT signature. We define 𝐺 Σ = ( 𝑉, 𝐸 ) to be the directed graph with 

𝑉 = 𝒮 Σ, 𝐸 = {(𝜎, 𝜎 𝑗 ) | ∃ 𝑐 : 𝜎 1 × · · · × 𝜎 𝑘 → 𝜎 ∈ 𝒞𝒪 Σ𝑛 +1 , 𝑗 ∈ [1 , 𝑘 ]} ∪ { (𝐴 𝑖 , 𝐸 𝑖 ) | 𝑗 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ]}.

A sort 𝜎 is called cyclic if there is a path with at least one edge in 𝐺 Σ that starts and ends with 𝜎 . A sort is called eventually cyclic if there is a nonempty path in 𝐺 Σ that starts with it and ends with a cyclic sort. 

Note, that by definition every cyclic sort is also eventually cyclic. 

Example 64. Consider Σndt from Table 1. Then the nodes in the graph are {𝐼, 𝐸, 𝐴 } and the edges are: 

• 𝐸 → 𝐴 (from the constructor c)

• 𝐸 → 𝐼 (from the constructor c)

• 𝐴 → 𝐸 (from an array sort to its element sort) Thus, the cyclic sorts are 𝐸 and 𝐴 (and 𝐼 is not eventually cyclic as it has no outgoing edges). 

Lemma 65. Let there be 𝜎, 𝜏 ∈ 𝒮 Σ such that there is a path in 𝐺 Σ from 𝜎 to 𝜏 . Then |𝜎 𝒜 | ≥ | 𝜏 𝒜 |.Proof : Write the (finite) path in 𝐺 Σ from 𝜎 to 𝜏 as

> 𝑖

| ≥ | 𝜌 𝒜 

> 𝑖 +1

> 𝑖

> 𝑠

and define:

Definition 66. Let ΣDT be a datatype signature and 𝒞 be a ΣDT -interpretation. For every 𝑥 ∈ 𝜎 𝒞 for some 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 ΣDT , we define the minimum index of 𝑥 , denoted dtIdx (𝑥 ), as follows: dtIdx (𝑥 ) = min {𝑖 ∈ N | 𝑥 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎,𝑖 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 )}

where 𝐵 is the Elem -sorted set with 𝐵 𝜏 = 𝜏 𝒞 for every 𝜏 ∈ Elem .

The underlying signature and interpretation in Definition 66 will always be clear from context. We will make use of the following lemma concerning dtIdx (·) and we include its routine proof for completeness. 

Lemma 67. Let ΣDT be a datatype signature and 𝒞 a ΣDT -interpretation. If 𝑥 = 𝑐 (𝑥 1, . . . , 𝑥 𝑟 ) ∈ 𝜎 𝒞 for some constructor 𝑐 : 𝜎 1 × · · · × 𝜎 𝑟 → 𝜎 ∈ 𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , then dtIdx (𝑥 ) = 1 + max {dtIdx (𝑥 1), . . . , dtIdx (𝑥 𝑟 )}.

∅, in contradiction. Hence, dtIdx (𝑥 ) ≥ 1. Let’s denote dtIdx (𝑥 ) as 𝑘 +1 for some 𝑘 ∈ N and ^𝑘 = max {dtIdx (𝑥 1), . . . , dtIdx (𝑥 𝑟 )}.By definition of dtIdx (·), 𝑥 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎,𝑘 +1 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) ∖ 𝑇 𝜎,𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ).Note that, by definition, 

𝑇 𝜎,𝑘 +1 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) = 𝑇 𝜎,𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) ∪{︁ 

𝑐 (𝑡 1, . . . , 𝑡 𝑘 )⃒⃒⃒ 𝑐 : 𝜎 1 × · · · × 𝜎 𝑘 → 𝜎 ∈ 𝒞𝒪 ΣDT ,𝑡 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 ,𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 )}︁ .

Since 𝑥 / ∈ 𝑇 𝜎,𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ), 𝑥 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 ,𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) for every 𝑖 ∈ [1 , 𝑟 ]. Since dtIdx (𝑥 𝑖 ) is the minimum index 𝑗 such that 𝑥 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 ,𝑗 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) and 𝑥 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 ,𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ), dtIdx (𝑥 𝑖 ) ≤ 𝑘 .Thus, by definition of ^𝑘 , ^𝑘 ≤ 𝑘 .Since 𝑥 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 ,dtIdx (𝑥 𝑖 )(Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) for every 𝑖 ∈ [1 , 𝑟 ], we know that 𝑥 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 ,^𝑘 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 )

for every 𝑖 ∈ [1 , 𝑟 ]. Hence, 𝑥 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎, ^𝑘 +1 (Σ DT |𝒞𝒪 ΣDT , 𝐵 ) and 𝑘 + 1 = dtIdx (𝑥 ) ≤ 1 + ^𝑘 .Thus 𝑘 = ^𝑘 and dtIdx (𝑥 ) = 1 + max {dtIdx (𝑥 1), . . . , dtIdx (𝑥 𝑟 )}. □

For the specific position 𝑗 ∈ [0 , ℓ − 1] where (𝜎 𝑗 , 𝜎 𝑗 +1 ) is an array edge, Lemma 87 gives a strict increase idx (𝑥 𝑘ℓ +𝑗 +1 ) > idx (𝑥 𝑘ℓ +𝑗 ). For every other step in the same traversal a constructor edge does not decrease the minimum index by Lemma 86, idx (𝑥 𝑘ℓ +𝑖 +1 ) ≥

idx (𝑥 𝑘ℓ +𝑖 ), and an array edge again yields a strict increase by Lemma 87. Hence each full cycle raises the minimum index strictly, i.e. ∀𝑘 ∈ [0 , 𝑀 − 1] . idx (𝑥 (𝑘 +1) ℓ) > idx (𝑥 𝑘ℓ ). It follows that idx (𝑥 𝑚ℓ ) > idx (𝑥 (𝑚 −1) ℓ) > · · · > idx (𝑥 0), so the elements 𝑥 0, 𝑥 ℓ, . . . , 𝑥 (𝑚 −1) ℓ have pairwise distinct idx (·) values and therefore are distinct. Since 𝑚 can be chosen arbitrarily large, we conclude that |𝜎 𝒜  

> ℓ

| = ∞. □

Now we can prove the intended lemma:

For technical reasons, we first define it as a partial function, but later (Lemma 83) prove that it is total; and, we define its range to be a superset of the domains of 𝒜 . B.6. Interpretation of Functions and Predicates 

The interpretation of function and predicate symbols in ℬ is defined in Table 23 and described as follows. 

Array operations. The symbols select 𝐴 𝑗 and store 𝐴 𝑗 are interpreted in the standard way, as read and update operations over functions. 

Datatype symbols. The interpretation of constructors and testers is fixed, as we require 𝒜 Σ𝑛 +1 to be a datatypes structure. As for selectors, their interpretation is fixed when applied to terms that are constructed by their corresponding constructors. When applied to different constructors, we maintain the connection to ℬ as induced by Ψ, or set them arbitrarily if no such connection is induced.

> (𝑐,𝑖 )

B.7. 𝒜 is a 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇

In order to show that 𝒜 is a 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇

-interpretation, we need to show that the relation ℛ𝒜 is well-founded. Recall that for every sort 𝜎 , 𝜎 𝒜 is defined to be ⋃︀ 𝑖 ∈N 𝜎 𝑖 . Thus, each element of 𝜎 𝒜 belongs to 𝜎 𝑖 for some minimal 𝑖 . For the next lemma, as well as for several subsequent lemmas, it will be useful to have a special notation for that minimal 𝑖 .

Notation 2. For every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ and every 𝑥 ∈ 𝜎 𝒜 , we define idx (𝑥 ) = min {𝑙 ∈ N | 𝑥 ∈ 𝜎 𝑙 }.

45 first case holds. WLOG, we can assume that for every 𝑖 ≥ 0, 𝑥 𝑖 +1 ℛ𝒜 𝑥 𝑖 by the first case (by renaming the indices). Thus, for every 𝑖 > 0, the first case holds and 𝜎 𝑖 ∈ Struct Σ𝑛 +1 . According to Lemma 62,

Base case 𝑖 = 0 . By the choice of 𝑗 0, 𝑥 0 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 0,𝑗 0 (Σ |𝒞𝒪 , 𝐵 ).

Inductive step. Assume 𝑥 𝑖 ∈ 𝑇 𝜎 𝑖 0+𝑖 ,𝑗 0−𝑖 (Σ |𝒞𝒪 , 𝐵 ). We denote by 𝑙 = min {𝑘 ∈ N | 𝑥 𝑖 0+𝑖 ∈

4. Contradiction: Therefore, there are infinitely many indices 𝑖 for which the second case holds, which contradicts the well foundedness of the natural numbers with the standard less-than relation. □

We can now prove that 𝒜 is indeed a 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-interpretation. 

Lemma 89. 𝒜 is a 𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σ

-interpretation. Proof : We have already seen that ℛ𝒜 is well founded in Lemma 88. Hence, we only need to show that • 𝒜 Σ𝑗 is an arrays structure for 𝑗 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ].• 𝒜 Σ𝑛 +1 is a datatype structure. For each 𝑗 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ], Lemma 61 shows that 𝐴 𝒜  

> 𝑗

is the standard functional interpretation of arrays, and we interpret select 𝐴 𝑗 and store 𝐴 𝑗 in the standard way for an arrays structure. As for Σ𝑛 +1 , Lemma 62 shows that ∀𝜎 ∈ Struct , 𝜎 𝒜 is interpreted in the standard way. Likewise, 𝒜 uses the usual tree-based interpretation of constructors, selectors and testers (see Definition 6). By construction, each selector and tester in Σ𝜑 𝑛 +1 obeys the requirements of datatypes structures. □

51 C. Decidability 

In this section, we prove Theorem 32. Let Σ be an NDT-signature and 𝜑 a flat Σ-cube, and recall the graph 𝐺 Σ from Definition 63. We prove a lemma similar to Lemma 65, but for 𝑇 𝜑 

> new

-interpretations. 

Lemma 91. If there is a path from 𝜎 to 𝜏 in the graph 𝐺 Σ (see Definition 63), then for every 𝑇 𝜑

One more ingredient that is needed for the proof of Theorem 32 is a generalization of Theorem 34 to multiple theories, which we prove here for the sake of completeness. Our proof uses Theorem 2.5 of [19 ] (which is an extended version of [ 20 ]) and Theorem 9 of [ 21 ] (the order-sorted Löwenheim-Skolem Theorem): 

Definition 93. Let 𝑉 be a set of variables and 𝛿 𝑉 a conjunction of equality literals and negations of such literals (i.e., equalities and disequalities) between variables in 𝑉 . We say that 𝛿 𝑉 is an arrangement of 

𝑉 if there exists an equivalence relation 𝐸 on 𝑉 such that 𝛿 𝑉 = ∧{ 𝑥 = 𝑦 | (𝑥, 𝑦 ) ∈ 𝐸 }⋀︀ ∧{ 𝑥 ̸ = 𝑦 |

(𝑥, 𝑦 ) /∈ 𝐸 }.

Theorem 94 (Many-sorted Löwenheim-Skolem Theorem). Given a many-sorted signature ˜Σ, a ˜Σ-theory ˜𝑇 , a satisfiable ˜𝑇 -formula Φ, and a ˜𝑇 -interpretation 𝒜 satisfying Φ, there exists a ˜Σ-interpretation 

ℬ satisfying Φ such that |𝜎 𝒜 | ≥ ℵ 0 implies |𝜎 ℬ| = ℵ0, for each sort 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 ˜Σ.

Lemma 95 (Thm 2.5 of [19]). Let 𝑇 𝑖 be a Σ𝑖 -theory, for 𝑖 = 1 , 2, such that ℱΣ1 ∩ ℱ Σ2 = ∅ and 

𝒫 Σ1 ∩ 𝒫 Σ2 = ∅. Let Σ = Σ 1 ∪ Σ2, 𝑇 = 𝑇 1 ⊕ 𝑇 2, and 𝑆 = 𝒮 Σ1 ∩ 𝒮 Σ2 . For 𝑖 = 1 , 2, let 𝜑 𝑖 be a flat Σ𝑖 -cube, and let 𝑉 = vars (𝜑 1) ∩ vars (𝜑 2). Suppose there exist a 𝑇 1-interpretation 𝒜 , a 𝑇 2-interpretation 

ℬ, and an arrangement 𝛿 𝑉 of 𝑉 such that 

• 𝒜 | = 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 ,

• ℬ | = 𝜑 2 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 , and 

• |𝐴 𝜎 | = |𝐵 𝜎 | for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝑆 .Then there exists a 𝑇 -interpretation 𝒞 such that 

• 𝒞 | = 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝜑 2 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 ,

• 𝐶 𝜎 = 𝐴 𝜎 for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ1 , and 

• 𝐶 𝜎 = 𝐵 𝜎 for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ2 ∖ 𝑆 .

Theorem 96 (Nelson–Oppen combination for 𝑛 theories). Let 𝑛 ≥ 2 and, for each 𝑖 ∈ { 1, . . . , 𝑛 },let Σ𝑖 be a signature and 𝑇 𝑖 a Σ𝑖 -theory. Assume: 

54 (i) For all 𝑖 ̸ = 𝑗 , ℱΣ𝑖 ∩ ℱ Σ𝑗 = ∅ and 𝒫 Σ𝑖 ∩ 𝒫 Σ𝑗 = ∅ (only sorts may be shared). (ii) For every 𝑖 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ] we have that 𝑇 𝑖 is stably infinite w.r.t. 𝒮 Σ𝑖 .(iii) The quantifier-free satisfiability problem for each 𝑇 𝑖 is decidable. Let Σ = ⋃︀ 𝑛 𝑖 =1 Σ𝑖 and let 𝑇 = 𝑇 1 ⊕ · · · ⊕ 𝑇 𝑛 . Then the quantifier-free Σ-satisfiability problem for 𝑇 is decidable and 𝑇 is stably infinite w.r.t. 𝒮 Σ.Proof : We prove the claim by induction on 𝑛 :

Base case (𝑛 = 2 ): According to Theorem 34, 𝑇 is decidable. We want to also show that 𝑇 is stably infinite w.r.t. 𝒮 Σ1 ∪ 𝒮 Σ2 . Let 𝜑 be a Σ-formula that is 𝑇 -satisfiable. We need to show that there exists a 𝑇 -interpretation 𝒞 such that 𝒞 | = 𝜑 and |𝜎 𝒞 | is infinite for every 

𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ1 ∪ 𝒮 Σ2 .Since 𝜑 is 𝑇 -satisfiable, there exists a 𝑇 -interpretation ^𝒞 such that ^𝒞 | = 𝜑 .W.l.o.g, 𝜑 = 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝜑 2, where 𝜑 1 and 𝜑 2 are conjunctions of Σ1-literals and Σ2-literals in 𝜑 , respectively. Let 𝑉 = vars (𝜑 1) ∩ vars (𝜑 2) be the set of shared variables between 𝜑 1 and 𝜑 2.We can define the following arrangement 𝛿 𝑉 of 𝑉 :

𝛿 𝑉 = ∧{ 𝑥 = 𝑦 | ^𝒞 | = 𝑥 = 𝑦 }⋀︁ ∧{ 𝑥 ̸ = 𝑦 | ^𝒞 | = 𝑥 ̸ = 𝑦 }

Note that ^𝒞 Σ1 |= 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 and ^𝒞 Σ2 |= 𝜑 2 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 . Since both 𝑇 1 and 𝑇 2 are stably infinite w.r.t. all their sorts, there exist: • a 𝑇 1-interpretation ℬ such that ℬ | = 𝜑 1 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 and for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ1 , |𝜎 ℬ| is infinite, and • a 𝑇 2-interpretation 𝒜 such that 𝒜 | = 𝜑 2 ∧ 𝛿 𝑉 and for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ2 , |𝜎 𝒜 | is infinite. By Theorem 94, we can further assume that |𝜎 ℬ| = ℵ0 for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ1 , and |𝜎 𝒜 | = ℵ0 for every 

𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ2 . In particular, for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ1 ∩ 𝒮 Σ2 , we have |𝜎 ℬ| = ℵ0 = |𝜎 𝒜 |.Thus, the conditions of Lemma 95 are satisfied. By Lemma 95, there exists a 𝑇 -interpretation 𝒞 such that 𝒞 | = 𝜑 1∧𝜑 2∧𝛿 𝑉 , and for every 𝜎 ∈ 𝒮 Σ1 ∪𝒮 Σ2 ,

|𝜎 𝒞 | is infinite. In particular, we also have 𝒞 | = 𝜑 .

Inductive step (𝑛 > 2): Assume the claim holds for 𝑘 < 𝑛 theories, and let 𝑇 1, . . . , 𝑇 𝑛 be Σ1, . . . , Σ𝑛 -theories, respectively, such that conditions (i), (ii), and (iii) of the theorem statement hold. Let Σ′ =⋃︀  

> 𝑛 −1
> 𝑖 =1

Σ𝑖 and 𝑇 ′ = 𝑇 1 ⊕ · · · ⊕ 𝑇 𝑛 −1. By the induction hypothesis, 𝑇 ′ is decidable and stably infinite w.r.t. ⋃︀  

> 𝑛 −1
> 𝑖 =1

𝒮 Σ𝑖 . Applying the IH once more to 𝑇 ′ and 𝑇 𝑛 , we conclude that 𝑇 ′ ⊕ 𝑇 𝑛 = 𝑇 is decidable and stably infinite w.r.t. ⋃︀ 𝑛 𝑖 =1 𝒮 Σ𝑖 . □

D.6. Discussion on the translation 

We describe various decisions made in our translation-based approach. 

D.6.1. The sorts 𝐴 ∘

Although trFormula (𝜑 ) does not introduce terms of the sorts 𝐴 𝑖 ∘ for 𝑖 ∈ [1 , 𝑛 ], those sorts are still required in Σ𝜑 . If we drop them, the translation may turn a satisfiable formula into an unsatisfiable one, thus violating Theorem 31. The following example makes this precise. Let 𝜃 be defined as follows: 

𝜃 := 𝑎 ̸ = 𝑏 ∧ 𝑥 = select 𝐴 (𝑎, 𝑖 ) ∧ 𝑥 = select 𝐴 (𝑏, 𝑖 ),

Where 𝑎 and 𝑏 are array variables of sort 𝐴 , 𝑥 is a variables of sort 𝐸 and 𝑖 is a variable of sort 𝐼 . 𝜃 is 

𝒯 N 𝐷𝑇  

> Σndt

-satisfiable. Assume we construct Σ𝜃  

> ndt

exactly as in Table 5 but omit the index sort 𝐴 ∘. The three component signatures that form Σ𝜃  

> ndt

are summarised in Table 27. Because 𝐴 ∘ is absent, the constructor 𝑐 1 now has arity 𝐸 * → 𝐴 * and hence only one selector. Then, the translation of 𝜃 would be 

trFormula (𝜃 ) = (︀ 𝑎 *̸ = 𝑏 * ∧ 𝑥 * = select 𝐴 ′ (𝑎 ′, 𝑖 *) ∧ 𝑥 * = select 𝐴 ′ (𝑏 ′, 𝑖 *))︀ 

∧(︀ 𝑢 (𝑎, 1) = select 𝐴 ′ (𝑎 ′, 𝑖 *) ∧ 𝑢 (𝑏, 1) = select 𝐴 ′ (𝑏 ′, 𝑖 *)

∧ 𝑢 (𝑎, 1) = 𝑠 (𝑐 1,1) (𝑎 *) ∧ 𝑢 (𝑏, 1) = 𝑠 (𝑐 1,1) (𝑏 *)

∧ 𝑎 ′ = 𝑓 𝐴 (𝑎 *) ∧ 𝑏 ′ = 𝑓 𝐴 (𝑏 *) ∧ 𝑎 * = 𝑔 𝐴 (𝑎 ′) ∧ 𝑏 * = 𝑔 𝐴 (𝑏 ′))︀ .

Since 𝑐 1 is the only constructor whose target sort is 𝐴 *, and it has only one argument (which is of sort 

𝐸 *), the four middle conjuncts imply 𝑠 (𝑐 1,1) (𝑎 *) = 𝑠 (𝑐 1,1) (𝑏 *), hence 𝑎 * = 𝑏 *. This contradicts 𝑎 *̸ = 𝑏 *,the first conjunct, so trFormula (𝜃 ) is 𝑇 𝜃

and 𝑠 (𝑐 1,2) (𝑏 *) may be equal without forcing 𝑎 * = 𝑏 *.

D.6.2. On The Third Lemma 

The motivation for L3(𝜑 ) is to ensure that for every array sort 𝐴 , there is a one to one mapping between the interpretations of free variables of sort 𝐴 ′ and those of sort 𝐴 *. 𝑓 𝐴 and 𝑔 𝐴 are asserted to be inverses of one another when restricted to the relevant domain, thus ensuring the 1-1 correspondence.
